<div id="review_581268358" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/581268358" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452041473p2/9456990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/581268358?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781857988215" href="/book/show/757009.Earth_Abides">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=classic-sf">classic-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=humanistic-sf">humanistic-sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer581268358" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6770745981165870046"><b>Earth Abides: Not with a bang, but a contented sigh...</b><br/>Originally posted at <a href='http://www.fantasyliterature.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantasy Literature</a>)<br/>You may have heard of pastoral SF (ala Clifford Simak), and this book may be best classified as post-holocaust pastoral SF, perhaps even "bucolic SF" (similar books include Leigh Bracket's Long Tomorrow and Pat Frank's Alas Babylon). Civilization is wiped out by a mysterious and never-explained virus, but our intrepid protagonist Isherwood Williams ("Ish" to his buddies) makes the best of a primitive ex</span>
  <span id="freeText6770745981165870046" style="display:none"><b>Earth Abides: Not with a bang, but a contented sigh...</b><br />Originally posted at <a href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantasy Literature</a>)<br />You may have heard of pastoral SF (ala Clifford Simak), and this book may be best classified as post-holocaust pastoral SF, perhaps even "bucolic SF" (similar books include Leigh Bracket's Long Tomorrow and Pat Frank's Alas Babylon). Civilization is wiped out by a mysterious and never-explained virus, but our intrepid protagonist Isherwood Williams ("Ish" to his buddies) makes the best of a primitive existence, first surviving alone by scavenging from the bountiful remains of grocery stores, hardware shops, and gas stations, and eventually gathering together a few stragglers to create a very basic family group of people that recreate a stripped-down version of society that is basically happy, healthy, and utterly lacking in introspection or regrets about the loss of modern civilization. Ish is a former graduate student, and therefore understands and treasures the knowledge represented by the books that remain in libraries, but as his children and grand-children are born into this new post-industrial world, their interest in the old world wanes with each successive generation. It becomes clear that life will go on without any reference to the past, as the title alludes to: "Men come and go, but earth abides."<br /><br />The book is filled with lush and loving descriptions of the steady, unperturbable rhythms of nature, weather, animals, and plants. The events of the story are sparse and could be summarized into just a dozen episodes, but the core of the story is the thoughts of Ish on the fall of civilization, and his gradual realization that it is gone and will not return, and his coming to acceptance of this.<br /><br />Thanks to reading this on my Kindle app, I discovered the handy highlight function and picked out a few representative passages:<br /><br /><i>That fact, when he thought of it, sometimes even made the Great Disaster seem beneficient – a magnificent wiping off of the slate which allowed man as a species to escape from of the aches and pains he had been accumulating for so many centuries, and start anew.<br /><br />Perhaps there were too many people, too many old ways of thinking, too many books. Perhaps the ruts of thinking had grown too deep and the refuse of the past lay too deep around us, like piles of garbage and old clothes. Why should not the philosopher welcome the wiping-out of it all and a new start and men playing the game with fresh rules? There would be, perhaps, more gain than loss.<br /><br />Must we not think then that this great civilization grew up not by men’s desires, but rather by Forces and Pressures. Step by step, as villages grew larger, men must give up the free wandering life of berry-picking and seed-gathering and tie themselves to the security (and drudgery) of agriculture. Step by step, as villages grew more numerous, men must renounce the excitement of the hunt for the security (and drudgery) of cattle-keeping. Then at last it was like Frankenstein’s vast monster. They had not willed it, but it ruled them all. And so by a thousand little surreptitious paths they tried to escape.<br /><br />Yes, the future was certain. The Tribe was not going to restore civilization. It did not want civilization. For a while the scavenging would go on – this opening of cans, this expending of cartridges and matches stored up from the past, all this uncreative but happy manner of life. Then at last, sooner or later, there would be more and more people, and the supplies would fail. There would perhaps be no quick catastrophe because cattle could be had for the taking, and life would go on.</i><br /><br />So why the 3 star rating? I enjoyed the opening section detailing his early months of survival and finally discovering a female companion to share his existence and then restart the populace. But the longer middle section takes it sweet time describing the bucolic daily existence of their growing family, and wouldn’t be out of place in any story of country existence other than short passages from an omniscient narrator. Certainly there are some setbacks and tragedies, but I found myself getting bored and skipping through a lot of this. <br /><br />However, the final section of the book, when Ish has reached a very old age and is treated as a sort of god or oracle by his progeny, has some very moving and melancholic reminiscing about the old world and the new, and is very profound. So in many ways the middle bit could be skimmed and you would still get the main effect and intent of the narrative. It remains a remarkable statement of a yearning for a simpler, pre-industrial existence, while still acknowledging the sadness and loss associated with the disappearance of all man’s hard-won knowledge and history.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6770745981165870046" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_133050003" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133050003" />
    <a title="Bob Ross" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4602704-bob-ross"><img alt="Bob Ross" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291166720p2/4602704.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/133050003?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Ross" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Ross" href="/user/show/4602704-bob-ross">Bob Ross</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133050003" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3491023952662434825">Picked this up because I heard this was the inspiration of Steven King's "The Stand". Written in 1949, it tells the story of a man named Ish that is one of the only survivors of a worldwide plague. Ish was based on Ishi, the last Indian who wondered out of the woods in the 1920's in California and was studied by Berkeley. Stewart taught at Berkeley so we see how he came up with this story.<br/><br/>I am puzzled why this is considered a classic and is so well reviewed, other than what I call "Star Trek syn</span>
  <span id="freeText3491023952662434825" style="display:none">Picked this up because I heard this was the inspiration of Steven King's "The Stand". Written in 1949, it tells the story of a man named Ish that is one of the only survivors of a worldwide plague. Ish was based on Ishi, the last Indian who wondered out of the woods in the 1920's in California and was studied by Berkeley. Stewart taught at Berkeley so we see how he came up with this story.<br /><br />I am puzzled why this is considered a classic and is so well reviewed, other than what I call "Star Trek syndrome", which is sci fi people had such a dearth of material back in the day they tend to gush over any author (or crappy TV show) that provides sci-fi rather than dealing objectively with the question of whether the book is actually good. There is no action or plot, the characters except for Ish are paper thin, and even he is arrogant and pretentious. The book starts out well with his story of how he survived and taking a tour of the USA to assess the damage done by the plague. But once Ish "settles down" the story crashes to a snooze.<br /><br />The story covers a few generations and is the life of Ish from beginning to death in a Brave New World. The author obviously pays homage to Huxley in the stilted language the narrator uses (almost a pretentious Shakespear-like dramatics) and his Ish is like the savage in BNW as they are both supposedly smarter than the people around them and can only watch with horror what is happening to people. Did this author think all sci fi had to be written in a weird shakesperian pretention because Huxley did it?<br /><br />As others have pointed out, even though it is refreshing that people do not act the way we expect in fiction, it does get tedious after 21 years people still are eating out of magic cans. This is usually pointed to as how this book is special. People are lazy and Ish doesn't really seem to like the survivors he meets. However, this starts to become unbelievable after year five or so. They do not learn to plant crops, make things, or do anything really. Kids are bored with learning..wow what a dilemma, make them learn like people have been doing for 50000 years! <br /><br />The author seems to not have a good grasp on the science (bad for this genre) as things last wayy! too long to be believable for the point he wants to make. (food, cars, razors, etc..are indestructable in this world)The worst sin for me, and what ruined the book is the fact that all this behavior is not organic to the characters, but seems shoehorned into the author's preordained conclusion people would revert to a primative state. Its ok to get to that conclusion if it is true to the story and organic, but it isn't here. Ish gives up on teaching because the kids are bored and are therefore hopeless, what kid wants to learn and be at school? People just eat cans for a generation because people don't want to learn new skills..there is no long term planning at all and it isn't believable. In Cast Away, Tom Hanks character was a mild manner Fed Ex guy that could spear a fish in three years on an island..people learn when they have to, they have kids in this book, where is the long term thinking? <br /><br />It may be ok to give this to students for a literary history of the genre, but I felt it heavy handed and stilted and dated. People do not act like real people, talk like real people (he actually calls his wife "oh mother of nations!" please) or make decisions like real people. I am sure that in the time it was written he may have had to sugarcoat an interacial union and tone down sex, but it comes across as absurd in the 21st century. (Ish the main character realizes after being involved with his wife Em for years after she is pregnant that she is black! He is the smart character btw) Barely finished it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3491023952662434825" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_52238116" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/52238116" />
    <a title="Amy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/563794-amy"><img alt="Amy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603051442p2/563794.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/52238116?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy" href="/user/show/563794-amy">Amy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everybody</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=became-a-part-of-me">became-a-part-of-me</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=plague">plague</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=just-good-literature">just-good-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/563794-amy?shelf=2009-books-read">2009-books-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer52238116" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8712545619788914761">No wonder this is a classic. It's a wonderful book. It's one of those books that tells a story with such a logical progression that it seems that anyone could have written it (but didn't). Yet, the wisdom this novel contains is the wisdom it takes the main character a lifetime to learn.<br/><br/>In the novel, a plague falls upon the earth, leaving behind a scant few survivors. Our hero, Ish, is one of the few survivors. One of his first inclinations is to travel across the US from California to New York t</span>
  <span id="freeText8712545619788914761" style="display:none">No wonder this is a classic. It's a wonderful book. It's one of those books that tells a story with such a logical progression that it seems that anyone could have written it (but didn't). Yet, the wisdom this novel contains is the wisdom it takes the main character a lifetime to learn.<br /><br />In the novel, a plague falls upon the earth, leaving behind a scant few survivors. Our hero, Ish, is one of the few survivors. One of his first inclinations is to travel across the US from California to New York to see who else has survived. Finding people (but nobody of like mind), he goes back home to San Francisco. If it would have been me, I would have searched the city for the nicest house to live in and scavenged the city for the nicest furnishings. However, Ish simply goes back to living in his parents' home.<br /><br />Over the course of time, people join Ish, he takes a wife, he has grandchildren and great grandchildren, and the world goes on, living off the leavings of the Old World. Ish is initially saddened that the people who make up this new tribe are not intellectually inclined as he is. They're ordinary folks with no great skills or inclinations toward religion. The tribe initially tries to keep connections to the past with church and school. However, these things fall away just as America has. When Ish asks the kids who they think made the world, they say that The Americans did. And they refuse to touch Ish's security-blanket-esque hammer because they see Ish as an American, one of the Old Ones, and perhaps a god. The new generations have primitively replaced religion with a set of new taboos and superstitions much like those people of early pre-history civilizations once did. <br /><br />While there is a logical progression to the book, people's logic can often be shocking. There is a point in the book where the tribal elders make a unanimous decision by secret ballot about the solution to a problem. The decision is based on fear and extreme conservatism. While the solution is a logical one, it bothers them on a moral level. And when disaster falls soon after, the elders first wonder if this is a punishment from god for their horrendous yet logical decision.<br /><br />I'm trying so hard not to give away the entire story here, but there are just so many great parts in this seemingly simple novel. In the end, you find yourself realizing the same things its taken Ish a lifetime to learn. What's really important in a civilization? Is it intelligence and invention or is it adaptability and happiness? Are intellectual people really more valuable than regular people in society? Is it better to bring back what was lost or is it better to forge a new future? </span>
  <a data-text-id="8712545619788914761" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_57616764" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/57616764" />
    <a title="Michelle Morrell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/127000-michelle-morrell"><img alt="Michelle Morrell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570031341p2/127000.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/57616764?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michelle Morrell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michelle Morrell" href="/user/show/127000-michelle-morrell">Michelle Morrell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/127000-michelle-morrell?shelf=post-apocalyptic-fiction">post-apocalyptic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/127000-michelle-morrell?shelf=x-read-2009">x-read-2009</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer57616764" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11696700419767835368">I started this book without a clear picture of when or where it was written, just knew it was "a classic." It started out with the stereotypical "lone survivor surveys the empty cities" scenes and moved on to a cross country jaunt to see what humanity survived. However, there was a scene where he came across a couple, a "negro" couple, whereupon he began to wonder if he should just stay there, become their master and have them provide for him. My first thought was "WTF?" My second was "When was </span>
  <span id="freeText11696700419767835368" style="display:none">I started this book without a clear picture of when or where it was written, just knew it was "a classic." It started out with the stereotypical "lone survivor surveys the empty cities" scenes and moved on to a cross country jaunt to see what humanity survived. However, there was a scene where he came across a couple, a "negro" couple, whereupon he began to wonder if he should just stay there, become their master and have them provide for him. My first thought was "WTF?" My second was "When was this written anyway?" and my third was, "Yeah, good luck with THAT." <br /><br />So that's when I realized this was written in the 40s. Paradigm shift for sure, I gained quite an appreciation for the novel. And it was really eye-opening how little the human animal changes, regardless of the technological advances around. The universal themes of solitude v lonliness, need for community and so on were indistinguishable from something that would take place today. Except for small "tells" here and there (and one big one!), I never would have guessed that it was 60 years old.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11696700419767835368" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_307240538" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/307240538" />
    <a title="Evan Leach" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7713825-evan-leach"><img alt="Evan Leach" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1384621842p2/7713825.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/307240538?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evan Leach" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evan Leach" href="/user/show/7713825-evan-leach">Evan Leach</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/13579895-earth-abides">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=1940-1949">1940-1949</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=american-literature">american-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer307240538" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11549756567216139579"><i>Earth Abides</i>, written in 1949, has a reputation as a landmark science fiction novel. It made Locus Magazine’s list of Best All Time Science Fiction and was a major inspiration for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/149267.The_Stand' title='The Stand by Stephen King' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Stand</a>. I know a number of people who really enjoyed this book, and my dislike for it certainly puts me in the minority. However, I was very disappointed by this one and found it to be a real slog.<br/><br/>In <i>Earth Abides</i>, a super virus nearly wipes out the human race in the late 1940s. Only a handful of survivors remain acr</span>
  <span id="freeText11549756567216139579" style="display:none"><i>Earth Abides</i>, written in 1949, has a reputation as a landmark science fiction novel. It made Locus Magazine’s list of Best All Time Science Fiction and was a major inspiration for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/149267.The_Stand" title="The Stand by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">The Stand</a>. I know a number of people who really enjoyed this book, and my dislike for it certainly puts me in the minority. However, I was very disappointed by this one and found it to be a real slog.<br /><br />In <i>Earth Abides</i>, a super virus nearly wipes out the human race in the late 1940s. Only a handful of survivors remain across the globe. One of these is Isherwood Williams, the story’s protagonist. Ish spends the first part of the book meandering around on his own, before mercifully finding another significant character to talk to: a woman named Em. This is good for Ish, but not so good for Em because my first problem with this book is that - <br /><br /><b>#1: Ish is Insufferable</b><br /><br />Isherwood Williams is not an easy guy to like. There are two central storylines in <i>Earth Abides</i>: the earth’s reversion to a natural state after the human population disappears, and the effect this disappearance has on the remaining humans. Once Ish and Em hook up with some other survivors, Ish quickly realizes he is the last person on Earth with a college degree and spends the rest of the book making sure we don’t forget it. George R. Stewart was a professor, and at times this book reads like an advertisement for the University of California. Ish’s fellow (non-collegiate) survivors are generally presented as dull, slow-witted individuals (with a few exceptions) incapable of long-term planning or deep thought. This makes Ish look like an elitist prig for the most part and it got very old, very fast. Poor Em has to put up with a lot of crap throughout this book but hanging out with Ish without braining him with a frying pan might have been her most impressive achievement. I can deal with a less-than-heroic protagonist (I loved <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/142458.Flashman__The_Flashman_Papers___1_" title="Flashman (The Flashman Papers, #1) by George MacDonald Fraser" rel="nofollow noopener">Flashman</a>, after all) if there’s a great story to be told but that brings us to problem #2…<br /><br /><b>#2: If the Earth Abides but Nothing Happens, Does Anyone Care?</b><br /><br />This isn’t a “kitten-squisher” of a book (Richard’s term) but at 373 pages it’s not exactly a novella, either. And there is very little excitement over that span. I thought the first 100 pages, where Ish just wanders around looking at shit, was truly excruciating and while the action picks up a bit from there, it is still a very slow moving book. It would be one thing if Stewart was using the book to highlight some truly novel (and unexpected) consequences of a disaster like this. But instead, he spends page after page describing things like metal getting rusty, plants growing where normally they would be weeded/trimmed back, etc. <br /><br />Now, not every book is going to read like a Michael Bay script. I have no problem diving into a slow moving cerebral book, even one with a pretentious jerk for a main character, if I’m being treated to some top-notch prose. Unfortunately, this leads to my third and largest problem with the book…<br /><br /><b> #3: The Writing! Drove Me Crazy!</b><br /><br /><i>Earth Abides</i> tells a story where civilization has effectively bit the dust and a small group of survivors attempt to start the whole thing over from scratch. There are definitely some Adam &amp; Eve themes in play here, and Stewart decides early on to adopt a semi-biblical tone. That’s fine in theory, but the results were truly painful to read. A sample:<br /><br /><i>"I have seen it again," Ish thought to himself. "The great pageant of the year! Now is the time of dryness and death. Now the god lies dying! Soon the rains will come, and then the hills will be green. At last one morning I shall look out westward, here from the porch, and I shall see the sun setting far to the south. Then we shall all go together, and I shall carve the number into the rock."</i><br /><br />Think about reading dialogue like that for over 300 pages. For me, this wore thin <b>very</b> fast and quickly became grating. Also, the multiple exclamation marks above are not at all atypical. There must be well over a thousand littering these pages. At times, it read like a comic book:<br /><br /><i>”What is the fascination?” said Ish to Em.<br />“Oh, don’t worry,” she said. It’s just the attraction of a stranger, something new. Isn’t that natural?”<br />“There is trouble ahead!”</i><br /><br />TO THE BATMOBILE!<br /><br />Honestly, the thing that put me over the edge in this regard is the fact that George Stewart can <i>actually write</i>. Towards the very end of the book, Stewart cuts the crap and just writes like a normal person, no biblical affectations or exclamation point fixation or anything. And once he does, the book is pretty darn good, even moving at times. But it was far too little, too late.<br /><br />Anyway. This book has an excellent reputation, and the book’s influence on the genre is undisputable. But I just can’t recommend it. <b>2 stars</b>, and much closer to a 1 for me than a 3.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11549756567216139579" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_825181933" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/825181933" />
    <a title="Chris Dietzel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19351209-chris-dietzel"><img alt="Chris Dietzel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1501356106p2/19351209.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/825181933?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Dietzel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Dietzel" href="/user/show/19351209-chris-dietzel">Chris Dietzel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer825181933" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15217614866404046389">This was excellent and lived up to all the good things I've heard about it. I loved the zoological and sociological look at the apocalypse rather than the typical us-versus-them or zombie apocalypse scenarios you seem to find so much of these days. Stewart did a remarkable job of making the daily lives of a few survivors not only seem interesting and extremely realistic (important for it to be a good book) but also provided philosophical insights and morals of human nature (the difference for me</span>
  <span id="freeText15217614866404046389" style="display:none">This was excellent and lived up to all the good things I've heard about it. I loved the zoological and sociological look at the apocalypse rather than the typical us-versus-them or zombie apocalypse scenarios you seem to find so much of these days. Stewart did a remarkable job of making the daily lives of a few survivors not only seem interesting and extremely realistic (important for it to be a good book) but also provided philosophical insights and morals of human nature (the difference for me between good books and great books).<br /><br />On a side note, I loved seeing how different two books can be that start with the same basic premise. Years ago, I created stories like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17800628.The_Man_Who_Watched_The_World_End" title="The Man Who Watched The World End by Chris Dietzel" rel="nofollow noopener">The Man Who Watched The World End</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18877920.A_Different_Alchemy" title="A Different Alchemy by Chris Dietzel" rel="nofollow noopener">A Different Alchemy</a> because I wanted a quiet end-of-the-world story. It was fascinating to see how much of a contrast Earth Abides is from my own books. Both focus on the social and environmental aspects of the end of man, but Stewart focuses on the rebuilding efforts of a few survivors while I focused on the inevitable ending of the last few who would one day fade away. The result are two starkly opposing views of the apocalypse. Stewart's is filled with hope and the slow re-establishment of man while mine is bleak and focuses on mankind's slow extinction--two similar starting points, two very contrasting endpoints. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15217614866404046389" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_175373599" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/175373599" />
    <a title="Daniel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/365191-daniel"><img alt="Daniel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1309463474p2/365191.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/175373599?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel" href="/user/show/365191-daniel">Daniel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/93270.Earth_Abides">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer175373599" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16727937970397851706">Sometime in the 10 days that it took me to read through this book, I decided that the title could be renamed to "Earth Bides"--as in the Earth bides its time, and so does George R. Stewart in his deliberate study of the decline of civilization following a world-wide plague. After a strong first part, Stewart's story dips into a depression of shallow character development and didactic storytelling. Agenda takes the fore, and Stewart's writing takes a manipulative turn as his character marionette </span>
  <span id="freeText16727937970397851706" style="display:none">Sometime in the 10 days that it took me to read through this book, I decided that the title could be renamed to "Earth Bides"--as in the Earth bides its time, and so does George R. Stewart in his deliberate study of the decline of civilization following a world-wide plague. After a strong first part, Stewart's story dips into a depression of shallow character development and didactic storytelling. Agenda takes the fore, and Stewart's writing takes a manipulative turn as his character marionette from one crisis to another. Put bluntly, the middle of 100 pages of this book were, at times, a bore.<br /><br />Still, "Earth Abides" is interesting for its time and place in fiction. Stewart wrote and published "Earth Abides" in 1949, so the society that gives way to nature and the passage of time is one that is devoid of cell phones, personal computers, internets, the multitude of plastic goods, disposable water bottles, a gi-normous nuclear industry and all of its lovely by-products, the civil rights movement, the Vietnam war, the sexual revolution, and other cultural movements and institutions that inform much of our modern thinking. The post-apocalyptic landscape that Stewart constructs is therefore very different from those that other artists depicted in the decades that followed. There is no radiation poisoning; no talk of world governments; no talk of the rest of the world in general. Without a nuclear arsenal to contend with--not to mention, any Cold War hang-ups about good vs evil, or all of the propaganda-prompted thinking that came with that period--his survivors are less attached to the world in any international sense, and thus free to consider their prospects for long-term survival in the context of an insular world that is less cynical than those proposed by other writers in later decades. (In fact, having read this, I am now interested in picking up other apocalypse-oriented fiction such as "The Plague," "Swan Song," "The Stand," "Hiero's Journey," etc, and comparing everyone's take on how it all ends).<br /><br />Also topical at the time is Stewart's depiction of people's lingering reliance on consumer goods such as matches and rifles and canned goods. His characters represent those who were immersed in the growing consumerism of the late 40s and used to its comforts. Once they assemble and agree to build a new life, they continue to seek out products and goods that they counted on for sustenance and industry. The main protagonist, Isherwood Williams, recognizes the limitations to this practice, and devotes himself to constructing a society that will be more self-reliant and capable--at least, in his eyes. Stewart plumbs some good insights from these parts of the story, and his portrayal of the different generations that follow the dissolution of civilization rings with enough truth to hold interest. Unfortunately, he is so keen to demonstrate his ideas that he resorts to didactic prose and plotting that is reminiscent of the dense writing that Ayn Rand poured into "Atlas Shrugged." The story slows considerably (one year occupies nearly 150 pages, whereas 21 passed by in less than twenty), and the characters devolve into flat game pieces that Stewart pushes around into arrangements that serve his agenda.<br /><br />"Earth Abides" is a long-winded elegy about mankind (and I must stress the male gender implied in that word, as female characters play only a minor role in the story) and its place in the world without the context of civilization. As stated, it is notable for its time and place in literature, and for the influence that it had on future writers such as Stephen King. If you do pick it up, consider this advice: set aside a healthy amount of time, and just power through to the last sixty pages, where Stewart rests most of his agenda, and writes some poetic and profound prose about the end of life as he knew it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16727937970397851706" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_18811695" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18811695" />
    <a title="Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/984874-thomas"><img alt="Thomas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1206673369p2/984874.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18811695?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/984874-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Teens and Older</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/984874-thomas?shelf=my-science-fiction-bookshelf">my-science-fiction-bookshelf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18811695" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6583567926626591630">This is my favorite novel of all time. I first read the story way back when I was in high school, so I can recommend this to young readers.<br/><br/>The story may well be the first post-apocalyptic novel of its kind - I know of no others that have proceeded it and I do not count <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/880695.H_G__Wells' title='H.G. Wells' rel='nofollow noopener'>H.G. Wells</a>' <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2493.The_Time_Machine' title='The Time Machine by H.G. Wells' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Time Machine</a> as in this category. Regardless, I consider this book to be the standard against which all other post-apocalyptic novels should be judged.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/53501.George_R__Stewart' title='George R. Stewart' rel='nofollow noopener'>George R. Stewart</a> is well suited to write this book as he has a</span>
  <span id="freeText6583567926626591630" style="display:none">This is my favorite novel of all time. I first read the story way back when I was in high school, so I can recommend this to young readers.<br /><br />The story may well be the first post-apocalyptic novel of its kind - I know of no others that have proceeded it and I do not count <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/880695.H_G__Wells" title="H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">H.G. Wells</a>' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2493.The_Time_Machine" title="The Time Machine by H.G. Wells" rel="nofollow noopener">The Time Machine</a> as in this category. Regardless, I consider this book to be the standard against which all other post-apocalyptic novels should be judged.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/53501.George_R__Stewart" title="George R. Stewart" rel="nofollow noopener">George R. Stewart</a> is well suited to write this book as he has a strong background in biology. He uses the premise of "what if the human race disappeared from the earth?" as one of the themes. Few survive the planet-wide plague that all be destroys humanity. Only a few survive and this is the tale of one of those people, from the days of the plague to his death (due to old age). It is a remarkable journey and well worth the read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6583567926626591630" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Bookman143" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5040081-bookman143"><img alt="Bookman143" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572108464p2/5040081.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2083700567?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bookman143" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bookman143" href="/user/show/5040081-bookman143">Bookman143</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9486800-earth-abides">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2083700567" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1744271556259922058"><strong>Thought-provoking post-apocalyptic novel</strong><br/><br/>Not an action-packed book and even quaint by today's standards, Earth Abides is a widely recognized classic of the genre with good reason. It puts one in mind of the Universal monster movies of the thirties and forties--enjoyable as classics but not facing the more stark and brutal realities portrayed in stories from our current time. So judging it by the time in which it was written, it was probably a ground-breaking book. But I think it has been much imp</span>
  <span id="freeText1744271556259922058" style="display:none"><strong>Thought-provoking post-apocalyptic novel</strong><br /><br />Not an action-packed book and even quaint by today's standards, Earth Abides is a widely recognized classic of the genre with good reason. It puts one in mind of the Universal monster movies of the thirties and forties--enjoyable as classics but not facing the more stark and brutal realities portrayed in stories from our current time. So judging it by the time in which it was written, it was probably a ground-breaking book. But I think it has been much improved upon by many succeeding post-apocalyptic novels. That or I am simply quite jaded.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1744271556259922058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_118740621" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/118740621" />
    <a title="Melissa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/287912-melissa"><img alt="Melissa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513280918p2/287912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/118740621?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa" href="/user/show/287912-melissa">Melissa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/287912-melissa?shelf=apocalyptastic">apocalyptastic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/287912-melissa?shelf=didnt-finish">didnt-finish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer118740621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13716101972014329897">I tried, but this book is too old fashioned for me. It reminds me of something Richard Matheson would write. The main character, Ish, drives me nuts. The women are all idiots according to him - but courageous, since they have the children, so that's okay! Apparently, after a plague wipes out nearly all of humanity, the difference between men & women can be summed up as: "She felt only in terms of the immediate, and was more interested in being able to spot her child's birthday than in all the fu</span>
  <span id="freeText13716101972014329897" style="display:none">I tried, but this book is too old fashioned for me. It reminds me of something Richard Matheson would write. The main character, Ish, drives me nuts. The women are all idiots according to him - but courageous, since they have the children, so that's okay! Apparently, after a plague wipes out nearly all of humanity, the difference between men &amp; women can be summed up as: "She felt only in terms of the immediate, and was more interested in being able to spot her child's birthday than in all the future of civilization," because Em doesn't care what month it is. Ish decides he "feels superior to her." It's not just Em, though, Ish thinks he's better than everyone! George is "a first-class carpenter" but "essentially stupid", dummy Maurine is always engaged in her "beloved tasks of housewifery", really, the list just goes on &amp; on. Everyone is an idiot, so he decides his son should lead the way because he can read. I believe his reasoning is that this child can always go to the library &amp; read about how to say, be a first-class carpenter. Now I of all people will agree that you can learn about a lot of things at the library, but maybe you shouldn't go around pissing on folks who can't read but who can still build houses. Self-help manuals don't work for everyone. <br /><br />There is one utterly perplexing scene early on. Em tells Ish she's pregnant and starts to cry, tells him, "I lied. Not what I said, what I didn't say . . . you're a nice boy. You looked at my hands &amp; said they were nice. You never even noticed that blue in the half-moons." Suddenly Ish sees her with a shocking new clarity &amp; notices her:<br />-brunette complexion<br />-dark liquid eyes<br />-full lips<br />-white teeth<br />-rich voice<br />-accepting temperment<br />and everything comes together in his mind. I am surely missing some crucial detail here. Is she an Irish setter? What the heck does this mean? I'm at a loss. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13716101972014329897" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peggyzbooksnmusic" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peggyzbooksnmusic" href="/user/show/2796355-peggyzbooksnmusic">Peggyzbooksnmusic</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2796355-peggyzbooksnmusic?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2796355-peggyzbooksnmusic?shelf=read-2016">read-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2796355-peggyzbooksnmusic?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2796355-peggyzbooksnmusic?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1675459964" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2900586492043917992">I don't read much science fiction but both my sister and a library friend recommended it. Wow! Not what I was expecting. It was written in 1949 the year I was born and was awarded the first International Fantasy Award. Imagine that most of Earth's population has been wiped out by an unknown virus. The main character, Isherwood "Ish" Williams, is one of the survivors and his emotional and physical journey is full of hope for himself a small group of men and women. <br/><br/>It took me a short while to get </span>
  <span id="freeText2900586492043917992" style="display:none">I don't read much science fiction but both my sister and a library friend recommended it. Wow! Not what I was expecting. It was written in 1949 the year I was born and was awarded the first International Fantasy Award. Imagine that most of Earth's population has been wiped out by an unknown virus. The main character, Isherwood "Ish" Williams, is one of the survivors and his emotional and physical journey is full of hope for himself a small group of men and women. <br /><br />It took me a short while to get used to the author's style of writing and the voice that he gave to Ish. There isn't much action in this novel but I was completely immersed in the story. Not only are we witness to how humanity reacts to this tragic event but we also see how Earth is changed with so few humans left. I had a feeling of almost claustrophobia during parts of the story. This definitely gave me much to think about in relation to our world today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2900586492043917992" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41064029" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15401666757921581109">3.0 stars. George R. Stewart's post-apocalyptic science fiction classic. I came into this story with incredibly high expectations and I think that may have tainted my experience with the book. It was well written with some very beautiful and haunting moments, but in the end it just didn't hold my interest enough to rate it higher. Good, but not great. <br/><br/>Winner: International Science Fiction Award for Best Novel (1951)<br/>Voted to Locus "All Time Best" Science Fiction Novels. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2947442343" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2947442343" />
    <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour"><img alt="J.M. Hushour" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442964894p2/17463213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2947442343?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.M. Hushour" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour">J.M. Hushour</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781857988215" href="/book/show/757009.Earth_Abides">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2947442343" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7340829236290782140">"At least," he thought, "life is quieter."<br/><br/>One of the golden age sci-fi greats, an early (1949) stab at the non-existent genre of the post-apocalypse. This is such a hoary trope now that one would be forgiven for not getting excited to read yet another goddamn post-apocalypse novel. But this one far surpasses, like many older genre works do, the crap that gets tossed off these days. <br/>Folks will be reminded much of Stephen King's <i>The Stand</i> whose first half is essentially a near-complete rip-off of </span>
  <span id="freeText7340829236290782140" style="display:none">"At least," he thought, "life is quieter."<br /><br />One of the golden age sci-fi greats, an early (1949) stab at the non-existent genre of the post-apocalypse. This is such a hoary trope now that one would be forgiven for not getting excited to read yet another goddamn post-apocalypse novel. But this one far surpasses, like many older genre works do, the crap that gets tossed off these days. <br />Folks will be reminded much of Stephen King's <i>The Stand</i> whose first half is essentially a near-complete rip-off of this book. A plague decimates humanity. The plot follows a young ecologist who happened to survive. The use of such a convenient narrator might sound a little too convenient, for drama's sake, but it really helps the novel stand out. This is because the novel isn't only about this guy wandering an empty America looking for other survivors, it is much more about how the world will change without us. It is finely detailed in its ecological descriptions and this, parallel to the real struggles and mostly failures of the surviving humans to make a go of it, really gives the story its meat and drive.<br />And these failures will surprise you. Nothing goes right for anyone. That's what makes it stand out. There is real emotional depth and people deal with real problems, not fucking zombies. A good example is the excruciating dilemma of a mentally retarded girl living among the survivors, who refuse to let her have children for the obvious, harrowing reasons. The whole book is littered with these very real-world dilemmas.<br />A fine novel!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7340829236290782140" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_28898417" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Huan-hua" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1332976-huan-hua"><img alt="Huan-hua" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/28898417?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Huan-hua" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Huan-hua" href="/user/show/1332976-huan-hua">Huan-hua</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">lovers of post-apocalyptic fiction</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Huan-hua by: </span>
      book club
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer28898417&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer28898417" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3667998185953959274">I loved this book. Someone else picked it for my book club a few years ago, and most of the people in the group hated it, so it's not for everyone--but I really enjoyed it. <br/><br/>I'm a sucker for postapocalyptic sci-fi, and this is subtler and gentler than the usual overblown, testosterone-laden books about hordes of zombies and cannibals and crazy-eyed survivalists with guns... it does a nice job of conveying the image of the world ending not with a bang, but a whimper. Most of humanity has been wipe</span>
  <span id="freeText3667998185953959274" style="display:none">I loved this book. Someone else picked it for my book club a few years ago, and most of the people in the group hated it, so it's not for everyone--but I really enjoyed it. <br /><br />I'm a sucker for postapocalyptic sci-fi, and this is subtler and gentler than the usual overblown, testosterone-laden books about hordes of zombies and cannibals and crazy-eyed survivalists with guns... it does a nice job of conveying the image of the world ending not with a bang, but a whimper. Most of humanity has been wiped out, and it's not necessarily every remaining person's first impulse to kill everyone else they meet.<br /><br />As a bonus, it's set in my hometown, Berkeley, so it gave me a bit of a thrill to read about Ish wandering the empty streets of the Berkeley hills, looking for his parents' house, or standing in the middle of the Bay Bridge, looking out on the Bay and the silent, still freeways; and later on, constructing bows and arrows among the rolling golden hills and live oaks of the familiar Northern California landscape.<br /><br />One of the other reviewers said they thought this book was too dated and it was hard to get past the descriptions of things like turning on the radio and waiting for the tubes to heat up, but I thought the book had actually aged really well. I didn't realize until I got about halfway through that it had been written decades back. <br /><br />The one part that really struck me as unpleasantly dated was Ish's visit to a black family's farm, when he thinks to himself how easy it would be for him to move in and lord it over them, because it had been the natural order for so long that they would just accept his domination and allow him to live like a king off their labors. The book, and Ish's thinking, are products of the time and place, I guess.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3667998185953959274" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_430882361" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/430882361" />
    <a title="Sher Free" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7154161-sher-free"><img alt="Sher Free" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374634555p2/7154161.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/430882361?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sher Free" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sher Free" href="/user/show/7154161-sher-free">Sher Free</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer430882361" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1097448870777560890">This book turned out to be both amazingly rewarding and incredibly exasperating, which is why I can’t make it a 5-star favorite even though I’m terribly tempted. Unfortunately, there was a huge chunk I really didn’t enjoy enough to justify doing that. Bear with me though because I just might change my mind. But before I get to the reasons why, anyone curious about reading Earth Abides should know that a good 2/3 to 3/4 was pretty much ALL observational narrative. Supposedly, although how could a</span>
  <span id="freeText1097448870777560890" style="display:none">This book turned out to be both amazingly rewarding and incredibly exasperating, which is why I can’t make it a 5-star favorite even though I’m terribly tempted. Unfortunately, there was a huge chunk I really didn’t enjoy enough to justify doing that. Bear with me though because I just might change my mind. But before I get to the reasons why, anyone curious about reading Earth Abides should know that a good 2/3 to 3/4 was pretty much ALL observational narrative. Supposedly, although how could anyone really know, this is more of a realistic vision of a post-apocalyptic world, at least much more than the Hunger Games variety oh so prevalent these days. As such we get drawn out accounts of the life cycles of ants or rats and other fauna, post-mankind of course, as well as the minutiae of mundane daily activities. Still, there were several inconsistencies for me, such as where were all the dead bodies? For a quick acting virus to seemingly wipe out the entire human race (in less than two weeks!), there were no bodies in the streets (save one in the very beginning) or in any of the homes which merely appeared to be abandoned. <br /><br />In addition every single character frustrated the hell out of me, including our protagonist, Ish. It seemed all he could do was bemoan the stupidity of those who survived even as he himself continued to be ineffectual – and all the while cultivating a god complex. One could see how that could occur, however, since everyone was so completely complacent, content to live off the remnants of civilization after “The Great Disaster“. Ish alone could see the dangers and futility of this, yet he was all too paralyzed by his cerebral nature and lack of leadership skills to do anything but worry. He was esteemed by his community of survivors as a visionary, if not mildly indulged, but certainly not taken seriously. Perhaps complacency is a more accurate portrayal of the response to a true apocalypse than we’ve been led to believe. I don’t know though, either it just didn’t ring true or I’ve been brainwashed into the vanity of human specialness and ingenuity. I’ll just say they really could have used an action oriented “Type A” person around, but nope, not a one in sight. I’m guessing the plague must have taken them out first. <br /><br />What's more it was sometimes difficult to get past the outdated views of race and gender presented in this book, which was written in 1949; although I think an attempt was made to portray Ish as somewhat progressive due to his status as a scholar and intellectual. Every time some comment or characterization served to remind me of the publication date, however, I had to tell myself he, through the lens of the author of course, was thinking and acting realistically as a white male product of the era. It was certainly a reminder that we have come a long way despite lingering attitudes and unresolved issues.<br /><br />Yet, regardless of these criticisms, some moments were beautifully written and captivating, arousing my curiosity enough as to want to see where it would all lead. And lead somewhere it most certainly did, enough to ultimately view all of my complaints as nitpicking, moreover, as imperative to the story’s denouement and essential message. It’s just too bad it was so protracted, or more likely that I am too much a product of our short attention span times to appreciate such a nuanced build up for what it was. The unending minutiae, the frustrations with the characters, even the race and sex issues to some extent, were crafted with such subtle intent as to come together in a powerfully emotional, meaningful climax. The long-awaited denouement was not only deeply satisfying, but possibly the most effective, beautiful, and emotionally wrenching of any book I have ever read. I came to realize how invested in Ish I had become. <br /><br />I can’t say much more without taking away the possibility of this same affective experience from anyone piqued enough to give this book a go. All I can say is everything that happens matters. It is a story about life, its ebbs and flows, how it endures and how it comes full-circle. Ultimately it is about the nature of man (yes, mostly man rather than woman, sorry to say, but again - 1949) and faith in humanity. But in its essence it is about faith in the earth, in both its constancy and change, and in its transcendence. Despite constant frustration throughout most of the story, I haven’t been able to let this one go. It is one of those books. The beauty and power of its conclusion will stay with me for a long, long time. If you read it, ignore all my nitpickings and stick with it. You won’t regret it. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1097448870777560890" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12349259202362877074">I did not like this, Dawn I am.<br/><br/>Boring. Antiquated. Boring. Asshole character. Boring. Did I mention boring?<br/><br/>Nothing happens. Plot goes no where. Everyone dies on page one... And that's pretty much it. I have no idea what the point of the rest of the pages was. <br/><br/>Main character, Ich, was a pompous douche.   <span id="freeText12349259202362877074" style="display:none">I did not like this, Dawn I am.<br /><br />Boring. Antiquated. Boring. Asshole character. Boring. Did I mention boring?<br /><br />Nothing happens. Plot goes no where. Everyone dies on page one... And that's pretty much it. I have no idea what the point of the rest of the pages was. <br /><br />Main character, Ich, was a pompous douche. I get that it's partially a matter of the time period the book takes place in, but it's also partially a matter of him being a know it all full of himself dick.<br /><br />And honestly.. The way he was portrayed.. He's around 20 (I think) when the disaster happens. By the end of the book he's an elderly man. His personality? Exactly the same as when he was 20. Meaning? The 20 year old version of Ich is written as a partially senile old man. Considering the fact that Stewart was 65 when he wrote the book, it doesn't surprise me.. I don't think he knew how to write a young man and so Ich came across seeming like he was 50 or 60 rather than 20. <br /><br />Regardless, this is all beside my point. My point is that I didn't like this book, it was ridiculously boring, and it fills me with rage. So glad to be done with it!</span>

<div id="review_1390195195" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1390195195" />
    <a title="Wayne Barrett" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21372390-wayne-barrett"><img alt="Wayne Barrett" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601067617p2/21372390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1390195195?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wayne Barrett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wayne Barrett" href="/user/show/21372390-wayne-barrett">Wayne Barrett</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21372390-wayne-barrett?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21372390-wayne-barrett?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21372390-wayne-barrett?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21372390-wayne-barrett?shelf=apocalyptic">apocalyptic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1390195195" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17762046320198655158">"Men go and come, but the Earth abides."<br/><br/>I couldn't help but conjure images of the Norse God, Thor, as Ish carried his hammer throughout the story and slowly came to be idolized by the following generations who followed. I can see how this apocalyptic tale inspired Stephen King to write 'The Stand'. <br/><br/>This book is a dated piece of fiction, but whether taking place in 1949 or in present day, one thing holds true, regardless of mans evolution and technology it could all come crashing down; our museum</span>
  <span id="freeText17762046320198655158" style="display:none">"Men go and come, but the Earth abides."<br /><br />I couldn't help but conjure images of the Norse God, Thor, as Ish carried his hammer throughout the story and slowly came to be idolized by the following generations who followed. I can see how this apocalyptic tale inspired Stephen King to write 'The Stand'. <br /><br />This book is a dated piece of fiction, but whether taking place in 1949 or in present day, one thing holds true, regardless of mans evolution and technology it could all come crashing down; our museums, Golden Gates, and colorful splashes of red. And yet our Mother Earth would spin on.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17762046320198655158" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_15237707" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15237707" />
    <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes"><img alt="Nancy Oakes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507555979p2/900340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/15237707?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy Oakes" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes">Nancy Oakes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780449213018" href="/book/show/1656242.Earth_Abides">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer15237707" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17186823264291486192">A very unique story in the genre of post-apocalyptic type novels, Earth Abides looks at the story of one man who survives a world-wide outbreak of a lethal plague. Instead of the hard-core nuts and bolts of survival, you get a somewhat idealistic view here, which, to me, makes this novel different than anything I've read in this genre. Quite good; I would recommend it if you're into this kind of stuff.<br/><br/>brief summary; no spoilers<br/>Isherwood (Ish) Williams is alone at a mountain cabin working on his </span>
  <span id="freeText17186823264291486192" style="display:none">A very unique story in the genre of post-apocalyptic type novels, Earth Abides looks at the story of one man who survives a world-wide outbreak of a lethal plague. Instead of the hard-core nuts and bolts of survival, you get a somewhat idealistic view here, which, to me, makes this novel different than anything I've read in this genre. Quite good; I would recommend it if you're into this kind of stuff.<br /><br />brief summary; no spoilers<br />Isherwood (Ish) Williams is alone at a mountain cabin working on his thesis, and is bit by a rattlesnake, causing him to stay inside and weather through the bite. After his health returns, he goes out to get food but finds no one around. After he does some searching, he comes across a newspaper that tells him what's going on: while he was up there, the rest of the country had met its end after a deadly plague. Little by little he comes across other survivors, and they group together in a little community near San Francisco. It is their story, and the story of Ish's fears for the future that dominate this book.<br /><br />Very very good read; I found myself stopping to think and do the "what-if" thing quite often. Recommended </span>
  <a data-text-id="17186823264291486192" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
